pawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Chess_plt45.svg/22px-Chess_plt45.svg.png)<br><br>![g2 white pawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Chess_plt45.svg/22px-Chess_plt45.svg.png)\\|   \\|   \\|   \\|   \\|   \\|   \\|   \\|8\\|<br>\\|7\\|7\\|<br>\\|6\\|6\\|<br>\\|5\\|5\\|<br>\\|4\\|4\\|<br>\\|3\\|3\\|<br>\\|2\\|2\\|<br>\\|1\\|1\\|<br>\\|a\\|b\\|c\\|d\\|e\\|f\\|g\\|h\\|<br><br>... and its pawn structure, known as the "Rauzer formation"\||

Chess strategy is concerned with the evaluation of chess positions and with setting up goals and long-term plans for future play. During the evaluation, players must take into account numerous factors such as the value of the pieces on the board, control of the center and centralization, the [pawn structure](/wiki/Pawn_structure "Pawn structure"), king safety, and the control of [key squares](/wiki/Key_square "Key square") or groups of squares (for example, diagonals, open files, and dark or light squares).

The most basic step in evaluating a position is to count the [total value of pieces](/wiki/Chess_piece_relative_value "Chess piece relative value") of both sides.[[34]](#cite_note-36) The point values used for this purpose are based on experience; usually, pawns are considered worth one point, knights and bishops about three points each, rooks about five points (the value difference between a rook and a bishop or knight being known as [the exchange](/wiki/The_exchange_(chess) "The exchange (chess)")), and queens about nine points. The king is more valuable than all of the other pieces combined, since its checkmate loses the game. But in practical terms, in the endgame, the king as a fighting piece is generally more powerful than a bishop or knight but less powerful than a rook.[[35]](#cite_note-37) These basic values are then modified by other factors like position of the piece (e.g. advanced pawns are usually more valuable than those on their initial squares), coordination between pieces (e.g. a pair of bishops usually coordinate better than a bishop and a knight), or the type of position (e.g. knights are generally better in [closed positions](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#closed_game "Glossary of chess") with many pawns while bishops are more powerful in [open positions](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#open_game "Glossary of chess")).[[36]](#cite_note-38)

Another important factor in the evaluation of chess positions is _[pawn structure](/wiki/Pawn_structure "Pawn structure")_ (sometimes known as the _pawn skeleton_): the configuration of pawns on the chessboard.[[37]](#cite_note-39) Since pawns are the least mobile of the pieces, pawn structure is relatively static and largely determines the strategic nature of the position. Weaknesses in pawn structure include [isolated](/wiki/Isolated_pawn "Isolated pawn"), [doubled](/wiki/Doubled_pawns "Doubled pawns"), or [backward pawns](/wiki/Backward_pawn "Backward pawn") and [holes](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#holes "Glossary of chess"); once created, they are often permanent. Care must therefore be taken to avoid these weaknesses unless they are compensated by another valuable asset (for example, by the possibility of developing an attack).[[38]](#cite_note-40)

### Endgame

Main article: [Chess endgame](/wiki/Chess_endgame "Chess endgame")

Example of zugzwang

|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
||a|b|c|d|e|f|g|h||
|8|![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chessboard480.svg/176px-Chessboard480.svg.png)<br><br>![c8 black king](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Chess_kdt45.svg/22px-Chess_kdt45.svg.png)<br><br>![c7 white pawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Chess_plt45.svg/22px-Chess_plt45.svg.png)<br><br>![d6 white king](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Chess_klt45.svg/22px-Chess_klt45.svg.png)|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |8|
|7|7|
|6|6|
|5|5|
|4|4|
|3|3|
|2|2|
|1|1|
||a|b|c|d|e|f|g|h||

The side to move is disadvantaged.

The endgame (also _end game_ or _ending_) is the stage of the game when there are few pieces left on the board. There are three main strategic differences between earlier stages of the game and the endgame:[[39]](#cite_note-41)

- Pawns become more important. Endgames often revolve around endeavors to [promote](/wiki/Promotion_(chess) "Promotion (chess)") a pawn by advancing it to the furthest [rank](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#rank "Glossary of chess").
- The king, which requires safeguarding from attack during the middlegame, emerges as a strong piece in the endgame. It is often brought to the [center](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#center "Glossary of chess") where it can protect its own pawns, attack enemy pawns, and hinder moves of the opponent's king.
- [Zugzwang](/wiki/Zugzwang "Zugzwang"), a situation in which the player who is to move is forced to incur a disadvantage, is often a factor in endgames but rarely in other stages of the game. In the example diagram, either side having the move is in zugzwang: Black to move must play 1...Kb7 allowing White to promote the pawn after 2.Kd7; White to move must permit a draw, either by 1.Kc6 [stalemate](/wiki/Stalemate "Stalemate") or by losing the pawn after any other legal move.

Endgames can be classified according to the type of pieces remaining on the board. [Basic checkmates](/wiki/Checkmate#Basic_checkmates "Checkmate") are positions in which one side has only a king and the other side has one or two pieces and can checkmate the opposing king, with the pieces working together with their king. For example, [king and pawn endgames](/wiki/King_and_pawn_endgame "King and pawn endgame") involve only kings and pawns on one or both sides, and the task of the stronger side is to promote one of the pawns. Other more complicated endings are classified according to pieces on the board other than kings, such as "[rook and pawn versus rook](/wiki/Rook_and_pawn_versus_rook "Rook and pawn versus rook")" endgames.

## History

Main article: [History of chess](/wiki/History_of_chess "History of chess")

### Origins

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/King_Khosrow_sits_before_the_chessboard%2C_while_his_vizir_and_the_envoy_of_Qannuj_are_playing_chess._Shahnameh%2C_10th_century_CE.jpg/310px-King_Khosrow_sits_before_the_chessboard%2C_while_his_vizir_and_the_envoy_of_Qannuj_are_playing_chess._Shahnameh%2C_10th_century_CE.jpg)](/wiki/File:King_Khosrow_sits_before_the_chessboard,_while_his_vizir_and_the_envoy_of_Qannuj_are_playing_chess._Shahnameh,_10th_century_CE.jpg)

[Sasanian Empire](/wiki/Sasanian_Empire "Sasanian Empire") King [Khosrow I](/wiki/Khosrow_I "Khosrow I") sits on his throne before the chessboard, while his vizir and the Indian envoy, probably sent by the [Maukhari](/wiki/Maukhari "Maukhari") King [Śarvavarman](/wiki/%C5%9Aarvavarman "Śarvavarman") of [Kannauj](/wiki/Kannauj "Kannauj"), are playing chess. _[Shahnama](/wiki/Shahnama "Shahnama")_, 10th century AD.[[40]](#cite_note-ME-42)[[41]](#cite_note-43)

Texts referring to the origins of chess date from the beginning of the seventh century. Three are written in _Pahlavi_ ([Middle Persian](/wiki/Middle_Persian "Middle Persian"))[[42]](#cite_note-44) and one, the _[Harshacharita](/wiki/Harshacharita "Harshacharita")_, is in [Sanskrit](/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit").[[43]](#cite_note-45) One of these texts, the _Chatrang-namak_, represents one of the earliest written accounts of chess. The narrator [Bozorgmehr](/wiki/Bozorgmehr "Bozorgmehr") explains that _Chatrang_, "Chess" in Pahlavi, was introduced to Persia by '[Dewasarm](/wiki/Sharvavarman "Sharvavarman"), a great ruler of India' during the reign of [Khosrow I](/wiki/Khosrow_I "Khosrow I"):[[44]](#cite_note-The_Sháhnáma_of_Firdausí:_Volume_VII-46)

> Dewasarm has fashioned this _[chatrang](/wiki/Chatrang "Chatrang")_ after the likeness of a battle, and in its likeness are two supreme rulers after the likeness of Kings (shah), with the essentials of rooks (rukh) to right and to left, with Counsellor (farzin) in the likeness of a commander of the champions, with the Elephant (pil) in the likeness of the commander of the rearguard, with Horse (asp) in the likeness of the commander of the cavalry, with the Footsoldier (piyadak) in the likeness of so many infantry in the vanguard of the battle
> 
> — Translation by Murray, 1913[[45]](#cite_note-47)

The oldest known chess manual was in Arabic and dates to about 840, written by [al-Adli ar-Rumi](/wiki/Al-Adli_ar-Rumi "Al-Adli ar-Rumi") (800–870), a renowned Arab chess player, titled _Kitab ash-shatranj_ (The Book of Chess). This is a lost manuscript, but is referenced in later works.[[46]](#cite_note-48) Here also, al-Adli attributes the origins of Persian chess to India, along with the eighth-century collection of fables [Kalīla wa-Dimna](/wiki/Kal%C4%ABla_wa-Dimna "Kalīla wa-Dimna").[[47]](#cite_note-Elephants_and_Kings-49) By the 20th century, a substantial consensus[[48]](#cite_note-Ancient_Board_Games_in_Perspective-50)[[49]](#cite_note-Players_and_Pawns-51) developed regarding chess's origins in northwest [India](/wiki/India "India") in the early seventh century.[[50]](#cite_note-Murray_(1913)-52) More recently, this consensus has been the subject of further scrutiny.[[51]](#cite_note-53)

The early forms of chess in India were known as _[chaturaṅga](/wiki/Chaturanga "Chaturanga")_ ([Sanskrit](/wiki/Sanskrit_language "Sanskrit language"): चतुरङ्ग), literally "four divisions" [of the military] – [infantry](/wiki/Infantry "Infantry"), [cavalry](/wiki/Cavalry "Cavalry"), [elephants](/wiki/War_elephant "War elephant"), and [chariotry](/wiki/Chariot "Chariot") – represented by pieces that would later evolve into the modern pawn, knight, bishop, and rook, respectively. Chaturanga was played on an 8×8 uncheckered board, called _[ashtāpada](/wiki/Ashtapada "Ashtapada")_.[[52]](#cite_note-54) Thence it spread eastward and westward along the [Silk Road](/wiki/Silk_Road "Silk Road"). The earliest evidence of chess is found in the nearby [Sasanian Persia](/wiki/Sasanian_Persia "Sasanian Persia") around 600 A.D., where the game came to be known by the name _[chatrang](/wiki/Chatrang "Chatrang")_ ([Persian](/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language"): چترنگ).[[53]](#cite_note-55) Chatrang was taken up by the [Muslim world](/wiki/Muslim_world "Muslim world") after the [Islamic conquest of Persia](/wiki/Islamic_conquest_of_Persia "Islamic conquest of Persia") (633–51), where it was then named _[shatranj](/wiki/Shatranj "Shatranj")_ ([Arabic](/wiki/Arabic_language "Arabic language"): شطرنج; [Persian](/wiki/Persian_language "Persian language"): شترنج), with the pieces largely retaining their Persian names. In Spanish, "shatranj" was rendered as _ajedrez_ ("al-shatranj"), in [Portuguese](/wiki/Portuguese_language "Portuguese language") as _xadrez_, and in [Greek](/wiki/Greek_language "Greek language") as ζατρίκιον (_zatrikion_, which comes directly from the Persian _chatrang_),[[54]](#cite_note-56) but in the rest of Europe it was replaced by versions of the Persian _shāh_ ("king"), from which the English words "check" and "chess" descend.[[note 3]](#cite_note-57) The word "[checkmate](/wiki/Checkmate "Checkmate")" is derived from the Persian _shāh māt_ ("the king is dead").[[55]](#cite_note-58)

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/KnightsTemplarPlayingChess1283.jpg/290px-KnightsTemplarPlayingChess1283.jpg)](/wiki/File:KnightsTemplarPlayingChess1283.jpg)

[Knights Templar](/wiki/Knights_Templar "Knights Templar") playing chess, _[Libro de los juegos](/wiki/Libro_de_los_juegos "Libro de los juegos")_, 1283

[Xiangqi](/wiki/Xiangqi "Xiangqi") is the form of chess best known in China. The eastern migration of chess, into China and Southeast Asia, has even less documentation than its migration west, making it largely conjectured. The word _xiàngqí_ (象棋) was used in China to refer to a game from 569 A.D. at the latest, but it has not been proven if this game was or was not directly related to chess.[[56]](#cite_note-59)[[57]](#cite_note-60) The first reference to Chinese chess appears in a book entitled _Xuánguaì Lù_ (玄怪錄; "Record of the Mysterious and Strange"), dating to about 800. A minority view holds that Western chess arose from xiàngqí or one of its predecessors.[[58]](#cite_note-61)[[59]](#cite_note-62) Chess historians Jean-Louis Cazaux and Rick Knowlton contend that xiangqi's intrinsic characteristics make it easier to construct an evolutionary path from China to India/Persia than the opposite direction.[[60]](#cite_note-63)

The oldest archaeological chess artifacts – ivory pieces – were excavated in ancient [Afrasiab](/wiki/Afrasiyab_(Samarkand) "Afrasiyab (Samarkand)"), today's [Samarkand](/wiki/Samarkand "Samarkand"), in [Uzbekistan](/wiki/Uzbekistan "Uzbekistan"), Central Asia, and date to about 760, with some of them possibly being older. Remarkably, almost all findings of the oldest pieces come from along the Silk Road, from the former regions of the Tarim Basin (today's Xinjiang in China), [Transoxiana](/wiki/Transoxiana "Transoxiana"), [Sogdiana](/wiki/Sogdiana "Sogdiana"), [Bactria](/wiki/Bactria "Bactria"), [Gandhara](/wiki/Gandhara "Gandhara"), to Iran on one end and to India through [Kashmir](/wiki/Kashmir "Kashmir") on the other.[[61]](#cite_note-64)

The game reached Western Europe and Russia via at least three routes, the earliest being in the ninth century. By the year 1000, it had spread throughout both the [Muslim Iberia](/wiki/Al_Andalus "Al Andalus") and [Latin Europe](/wiki/Latin_Christianity "Latin Christianity").[[62]](#cite_note-Hooper_&_Whyld_(1992),_pp._173–75-65) A Latin poem called _[Versus de scachis](/wiki/Versus_de_scachis "Versus de scachis")_ ("Verses on Chess") dated to the late 10th century, has been preserved at [Einsiedeln Abbey](/wiki/Einsiedeln_Abbey "Einsiedeln Abbey") in Switzerland.

### 1200–1700: Origins of the modern game

The game of chess was then played and known in all European countries. A famous 13th-century Spanish manuscript covering chess, [backgammon](/wiki/Backgammon "Backgammon"), and [dice](/wiki/Dice "Dice") is known as the _[Libro de los juegos](/wiki/Libro_de_los_juegos "Libro de los juegos")_, which is the earliest [European](/wiki/Europe "Europe") treatise on chess as well as being the oldest document on European [tables games](/wiki/Tables_games "Tables games"). The rules were fundamentally similar to those of the Arabic [shatranj](/wiki/Shatranj "Shatranj"). The differences were mostly in the use of a checkered board instead of a plain monochrome board used by Arabs and the habit of allowing some or all pawns to make an initial double step. In some regions, the queen, which had replaced the [wazir](/wiki/Wazir_(chess) "Wazir (chess)"), or the king could also make an initial two-square leap under some conditions.[[63]](#cite_note-66)

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Lucena110.png/220px-Lucena110.png)](/wiki/File:Lucena110.png)

A tactical puzzle from [Lucena's](/wiki/Luis_Ram%C3%ADrez_de_Lucena "Luis Ramírez de Lucena") 1497 book

Around 1200, the rules of shatranj started to be modified in Europe, culminating, several major changes later, in the emergence of modern chess practically as it is known today.[[64]](#cite_note-67) A major change was the modern piece movement rules, which began to appear in intellectual circles in [Valencia](/wiki/Valencia "Valencia"), Spain, around 1475,[[note 4]](#cite_note-70) which established the foundations and brought it very close to current chess. These new rules then were quickly adopted in Italy and Southern France before diffusing into the rest of Europe.[[67]](#cite_note-71)[[68]](#cite_note-nauiua-72) Pawns gained the ability to advance two squares on their first move, while bishops and queens acquired their modern movement powers. The [queen](/wiki/Queen_(chess) "Queen (chess)") replaced the earlier [vizier](/wiki/Vizier#Influence_on_chess "Vizier") chess piece toward the end of the 10th century and by the 15th century had become the most powerful piece;[[69]](#cite_note-73) in light of that, modern chess was often referred to at the time as "Queen's Chess" or "Mad Queen Chess".[[70]](#cite_note-74) Castling, derived from the "king's leap", usually in combination with a pawn or rook move to bring the king to safety, was introduced. These new rules quickly spread throughout Western Europe.

Writings about [chess theory](/wiki/Chess_theory "Chess theory") began to appear in the late 15th century. An [anonymous treatise on chess of 1490](/wiki/G%C3%B6ttingen_manuscript "Göttingen manuscript") with the first part containing some openings and the second 30 endgames is deposited in the library of the [University of Göttingen](/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen "University of Göttingen").[[71]](#cite_note-:1-75) The book _El Libro dels jochs partitis dels schachs en nombre de 100_ was written by [Francesc Vicent](/wiki/Francesc_Vicent "Francesc Vicent") in [Segorbe](/wiki/Segorbe "Segorbe") in 1495, but no copy of this work has survived.[[71]](#cite_note-:1-75) The _Repetición de Amores y Arte de Ajedrez_ (_Repetition of Love and the Art of Playing Chess_) by [Spanish](/wiki/Spanish_people "Spanish people") churchman [Luis Ramírez de Lucena](/wiki/Luis_Ram%C3%ADrez_de_Lucena "Luis Ramírez de Lucena") was published in [Salamanca](/wiki/Salamanca "Salamanca") in 1497.[[68]](#cite_note-nauiua-72) Lucena and later masters like Portuguese [Pedro Damiano](/wiki/Pedro_Damiano "Pedro Damiano"), Italians [Giovanni Leonardo Di Bona](/wiki/Giovanni_Leonardo_Di_Bona "Giovanni Leonardo Di Bona"), [Giulio Cesare Polerio](/wiki/Giulio_Cesare_Polerio "Giulio Cesare Polerio") and [Gioachino Greco](/wiki/Gioachino_Greco "Gioachino Greco"), and Spanish bishop [Ruy López de Segura](/wiki/Ruy_L%C3%B3pez_de_Segura "Ruy López de Segura") developed elements of [opening theory](#Opening) and started to analyze simple [endgames](#Endgame).

### 1700–1873: Romantic era

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Immortal_game_animation.gif)](/wiki/File:Immortal_game_animation.gif)

The "[Immortal Game](/wiki/Immortal_Game "Immortal Game")", Anderssen vs. Kieseritzky, 1851

In the 18th century, the center of European chess life moved from Southern Europe to mainland France. The two most important French masters were [François-André Danican Philidor](/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois-Andr%C3%A9_Danican_Philidor "François-André Danican Philidor"), a musician by profession, who discovered the importance of pawns for chess strategy, and later [Louis-Charles Mahé de La Bourdonnais](/wiki/Louis-Charles_Mah%C3%A9_de_La_Bourdonnais "Louis-Charles Mahé de La Bourdonnais"), who won a famous series of matches against Irish master [Alexander McDonnell](/wiki/Alexander_McDonnell_(chess_player) "Alexander McDonnell (chess player)") in 1834.[[72]](#cite_note-76) Centers of chess activity in this period were [coffee houses](/wiki/Coffee_house "Coffee house") in major European cities like _[Café de la Régence](/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_la_R%C3%A9gence "Café de la Régence")_ in Paris and _[Simpson's Divan](/wiki/Simpson%27s-in-the-Strand "Simpson's-in-the-Strand")_ in London.[[73]](#cite_note-77)[[74]](#cite_note-78)

At the same time, the intellectual movement of [romanticism](/wiki/Romanticism "Romanticism") had had a far-reaching impact on chess, with aesthetics and tactical beauty being held in higher regard than objective soundness and strategic planning. As a result, virtually all games began with the [Open Game](/wiki/Open_Game "Open Game"), and it was considered unsportsmanlike to decline gambits that invited tactical play such as the [King's Gambit](/wiki/King%27s_Gambit "King's Gambit") and the [Evans Gambit](/wiki/Evans_Gambit "Evans Gambit").[[75]](#cite_note-Shenk,_2007-79) This chess philosophy is known as [Romantic chess](/wiki/Romantic_chess "Romantic chess"), and a sharp, tactical style consistent with the principles of chess romanticism was predominant until the late 19th century.[[76]](#cite_note-80)

[The rules concerning stalemate](/wiki/Stalemate#History_of_the_stalemate_rule "Stalemate") were finalized in the early 19th century. Also in the 19th century, the convention that White moves first was established (formerly either White or Black could move first). Finally, the rules around castling and en passant captures were standardized – variations in these rules persisted in Italy until the late 19th century. The resulting standard game is sometimes referred to as _Western chess_[[77]](#cite_note-81) or _international chess_,[[78]](#cite_note-82) particularly in Asia where other games of the chess family such as [xiangqi](/wiki/Xiangqi "Xiangqi") are prevalent. Since the 19th century, the only rule changes, such as the establishment of the correct procedure for claiming a draw by repetition, have been technical in nature.

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/ChessNL186X.jpg/220px-ChessNL186X.jpg)](/wiki/File:ChessNL186X.jpg)

Chess in the Netherlands (1864)

As the 19th century progressed, chess organization developed quickly. Many [chess clubs](/wiki/Chess_club "Chess club"), chess books, and chess journals appeared. There were correspondence matches between cities; for example, the London Chess Club played against the [Edinburgh](/wiki/Edinburgh "Edinburgh") Chess Club in 1824.[[79]](#cite_note-83) [Chess problems](/wiki/Chess_problems "Chess problems") became a regular part of 19th-century newspapers; [Bernhard Horwitz](/wiki/Bernhard_Horwitz "Bernhard Horwitz"), [Josef Kling](/wiki/Josef_Kling "Josef Kling"), and [Samuel Loyd](/wiki/Samuel_Loyd "Samuel Loyd") composed some of the most influential problems. In 1843, [von der Lasa](/wiki/Tassilo_von_Heydebrand_und_der_Lasa "Tassilo von Heydebrand und der Lasa") published his and [Bilguer's](/wiki/Paul_Rudolf_von_Bilguer "Paul Rudolf von Bilguer") _[Handbuch des Schachspiels](/wiki/Handbuch_des_Schachspiels "Handbuch des Schachspiels")_ (_Handbook of Chess_), the first comprehensive manual of chess theory.

The first modern chess tournament was organized by [Howard Staunton](/wiki/Howard_Staunton "Howard Staunton"), a leading English chess player, and was [held in London in 1851](/wiki/London_1851_chess_tournament "London 1851 chess tournament"). It was won by the German [Adolf Anderssen](/wiki/Adolf_Anderssen "Adolf Anderssen"), who was hailed as the leading chess master. His brilliant, energetic attacking style was [typical for the time](/wiki/Romantic_chess "Romantic chess").[[80]](#cite_note-84)[[81]](#cite_note-85) Sparkling games like Anderssen's [Immortal Game](/wiki/Immortal_Game "Immortal Game") and [Evergreen Game](/wiki/Evergreen_Game "Evergreen Game") or [Morphy's](/wiki/Paul_Morphy "Paul Morphy") "[Opera Game](/wiki/Morphy_versus_the_Duke_of_Brunswick_and_Count_Isouard "Morphy versus the Duke of Brunswick and Count Isouard")" were regarded as the highest possible summit of the art of chess.[[82]](#cite_note-86)

Deeper insight into the nature of chess came with the American [Paul Morphy](/wiki/Paul_Morphy "Paul Morphy"), an extraordinary [chess prodigy](/wiki/Chess_prodigy "Chess prodigy"). Morphy won against all important competitors (except Staunton, who refused to play), including Anderssen, during his short chess career between 1857 and 1863. Morphy's success stemmed from a combination of brilliant attacks and sound strategy; he intuitively knew how to prepare attacks.[[83]](#cite_note-87)

### 1873–1945: Birth of a sport

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Wilhelm_Steinitz2.jpg/150px-Wilhelm_Steinitz2.jpg)](/wiki/File:Wilhelm_Steinitz2.jpg)

[Wilhelm Steinitz](/wiki/Wilhelm_Steinitz "Wilhelm Steinitz"), the first official [World Chess Champion](/wiki/World_Chess_Champion "World Chess Champion"), from 1886 to 1894

[Prague](/wiki/Prague "Prague")-born [Wilhelm Steinitz](/wiki/Wilhelm_Steinitz "Wilhelm Steinitz") laid the foundations for a scientific approach to the game, the art of breaking a position down into components[[84]](#cite_note-88) and preparing correct plans.[[85]](#cite_note-89) In addition to his theoretical achievements, Steinitz founded an important tradition: his triumph over the leading German master [Johannes Zukertort](/wiki/Johannes_Zukertort "Johannes Zukertort") in 1886 is regarded as the first official [World Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship "World Chess Championship"). This win marked a stylistic transition at the highest levels of chess from an attacking, tactical style predominant in the Romantic era to a more positional, strategic style introduced to the chess world by Steinitz. Steinitz lost his crown in 1894 to a much younger player, the German mathematician [Emanuel Lasker](/wiki/Emanuel_Lasker "Emanuel Lasker"), who maintained this title for 27 years, the longest tenure of any world champion.[[86]](#cite_note-90)

After the end of the 19th century, the number of master tournaments and matches held annually quickly grew. The first [Olympiad](/wiki/Chess_Olympiad "Chess Olympiad") was held in Paris in 1924, and [FIDE](/wiki/FIDE "FIDE") was founded initially for the purpose of organizing that event. In 1927, the [Women's World Chess Championship](/wiki/Women%27s_World_Chess_Championship "Women's World Chess Championship") was established; the first to hold the title was Czech-English master [Vera Menchik](/wiki/Vera_Menchik "Vera Menchik").[[87]](#cite_note-91)

A prodigy from Cuba, [José Raúl Capablanca](/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Ra%C3%BAl_Capablanca "José Raúl Capablanca"), known for his skill in endgames, won the World Championship from Lasker in 1921. Capablanca was undefeated in tournament play for eight years, from 1916 to 1924. His successor (1927) was the Russian-French [Alexander Alekhine](/wiki/Alexander_Alekhine "Alexander Alekhine"), a strong attacking player who died as the world champion in 1946. Alekhine briefly lost the title to Dutch player [Max Euwe](/wiki/Max_Euwe "Max Euwe") in 1935 and regained it two years later.[[88]](#cite_note-92)

In the [interwar period](/wiki/Interwar_period "Interwar period"), chess was revolutionized by the new theoretical school of so-called [hypermodernists](/wiki/Hypermodernism_(chess) "Hypermodernism (chess)") like [Aron Nimzowitsch](/wiki/Aron_Nimzowitsch "Aron Nimzowitsch") and [Richard Réti](/wiki/Richard_R%C3%A9ti "Richard Réti"). They advocated controlling the [center](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#center "Glossary of chess") of the board with distant pieces rather than with pawns, thus inviting opponents to occupy the center with pawns, which become objects of attack.[[89]](#cite_note-93)

### 1945–1990: Post-World War II era

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Mikhail_Botvinnik_1962.jpg/180px-Mikhail_Botvinnik_1962.jpg)](/wiki/File:Mikhail_Botvinnik_1962.jpg)

[Mikhail Botvinnik](/wiki/Mikhail_Botvinnik "Mikhail Botvinnik"), the first post-war World Champion

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Bobby_Fischer_1960_in_Leipzig.jpg/180px-Bobby_Fischer_1960_in_Leipzig.jpg)](/wiki/File:Bobby_Fischer_1960_in_Leipzig.jpg)

[Bobby Fischer](/wiki/Bobby_Fischer "Bobby Fischer"), World Champion from 1972 to 1975

After the death of Alekhine, a new World Champion was sought. FIDE, which has controlled the title since then, ran a tournament of elite players. The winner of the [1948 tournament](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_1948 "World Chess Championship 1948") was Russian [Mikhail Botvinnik](/wiki/Mikhail_Botvinnik "Mikhail Botvinnik"). In 1950 FIDE established a system of titles, conferring the titles of [Grandmaster](/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess) "Grandmaster (chess)") and International Master on 27 players. (Some sources state that in 1914 the title of chess Grandmaster was first formally conferred by Tsar [Nicholas II of Russia](/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia "Nicholas II of Russia") to Lasker, Capablanca, Alekhine, [Tarrasch](/wiki/Siegbert_Tarrasch "Siegbert Tarrasch"), and [Marshall](/wiki/Frank_Marshall_(chess_player) "Frank Marshall (chess player)"), but this is a disputed claim.[[note 5]](#cite_note-94))

Botvinnik started an era of [Soviet](/wiki/Soviet_Union "Soviet Union") dominance in the chess world, which mainly through the Soviet government's politically inspired efforts to demonstrate intellectual superiority over the West[[90]](#cite_note-95)[[91]](#cite_note-96) stood almost uninterrupted for more than a half-century. Until the [dissolution of the Soviet Union](/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Soviet_Union "Dissolution of the Soviet Union"), there was only one non-Soviet champion, American [Bobby Fischer](/wiki/Bobby_Fischer "Bobby Fischer") (champion 1972–1975).[[92]](#cite_note-97) Botvinnik also revolutionized [opening theory](/wiki/Chess_theory#Opening_theory "Chess theory"). Previously, Black strove for equality, attempting to neutralize White's [first-move advantage](/wiki/First-move_advantage_in_chess "First-move advantage in chess"). As Black, Botvinnik strove for the initiative from the beginning.[[93]](#cite_note-98) In the previous informal system of World Championships, the current champion decided which challenger he would play for the title and the challenger was forced to seek sponsors for the match. FIDE set up a new system of qualifying tournaments and matches. The world's strongest players were seeded into [Interzonal](/wiki/Interzonal "Interzonal") tournaments, where they were joined by players who had qualified from Zonal tournaments. The leading finishers in these Interzonals would go through the "[Candidates](/wiki/Candidates_Tournament "Candidates Tournament")" stage, which was initially a tournament, and later a series of knockout matches. The winner of the Candidates would then play the reigning champion for the title. A champion defeated in a match had a right to play a rematch a year later. This system operated on a three-year cycle. Botvinnik participated in championship matches over a period of fifteen years. He won the world championship tournament in 1948 and retained the title in tied matches in 1951 and 1954. In 1957, he lost to [Vasily Smyslov](/wiki/Vasily_Smyslov "Vasily Smyslov"), but regained the title in a rematch in 1958. In 1960, he lost the title to the 23-year-old Latvian prodigy [Mikhail Tal](/wiki/Mikhail_Tal "Mikhail Tal"), an accomplished [tactician](/wiki/Chess_tactics "Chess tactics") and attacking player who is widely regarded as one of the most creative players ever,[[94]](#cite_note-99) hence his nickname "the magician from Riga". Botvinnik again regained the title in a rematch in 1961.

Following the 1961 event, FIDE abolished the automatic right of a deposed champion to a rematch, and the next champion, Armenian [Tigran Petrosian](/wiki/Tigran_Petrosian "Tigran Petrosian"), a player renowned for his defensive and positional skills, held the title for two cycles, 1963–1969. His successor, [Boris Spassky](/wiki/Boris_Spassky "Boris Spassky") from Russia (champion 1969–1972), won games in both positional and sharp tactical style.[[95]](#cite_note-100) The next championship, the so-called [Match of the Century](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_1972 "World Chess Championship 1972"), saw the first non-Soviet challenger since World War II, American [Bobby Fischer](/wiki/Bobby_Fischer "Bobby Fischer"). Fischer defeated his opponents in the [Candidates](/wiki/Candidates_Tournament "Candidates Tournament") matches by unheard-of margins, and convincingly defeated Spassky for the world championship. The match was followed closely by news media of the day, leading to a surge in popularity for chess; it also held significant political importance at the height of the [Cold War](/wiki/Cold_War "Cold War"), with the match being seen by both sides as a [microcosm](/wiki/Microcosm%E2%80%93macrocosm_analogy "Microcosm–macrocosm analogy") of the conflict between East and West.[[96]](#cite_note-101) In 1975, however, Fischer refused to defend his title against Soviet [Anatoly Karpov](/wiki/Anatoly_Karpov "Anatoly Karpov") when he was unable to reach agreement on conditions with FIDE, and Karpov obtained the title by default.[[97]](#cite_note-102) Fischer modernized many aspects of chess, especially by extensively preparing openings.[[98]](#cite_note-103)

Karpov defended his title twice against [Viktor Korchnoi](/wiki/Viktor_Korchnoi "Viktor Korchnoi") and dominated the 1970s and early 1980s with a string of tournament successes.[[99]](#cite_note-104) In the [1984 World Chess Championship](/wiki/1984_World_Chess_Championship "1984 World Chess Championship"), Karpov faced his toughest challenge to date, the young [Garry Kasparov](/wiki/Garry_Kasparov "Garry Kasparov") from [Baku](/wiki/Baku "Baku"), [Soviet Azerbaijan](/wiki/Soviet_Azerbaijan "Soviet Azerbaijan"). The match was aborted in controversial circumstances after 5 months and 48 games with Karpov leading by 5 wins to 3, but evidently exhausted; many commentators believed Kasparov, who had won the last two games, would have won the match had it continued. Kasparov won the [1985 rematch](/wiki/1985_World_Chess_Championship "1985 World Chess Championship"). Kasparov and Karpov contested three further closely fought matches in 1986, 1987 and 1990, Kasparov winning them all.[[100]](#cite_note-105) Kasparov became the dominant figure of world chess from the mid-1980s until his retirement from competition in 2005.

#### Beginnings of chess technology

Chess-playing computer programs (later known as [chess engines](/wiki/Chess_engines "Chess engines")) began to appear in the 1960s. In 1970, the first major computer chess tournament, the [North American Computer Chess Championship](/wiki/North_American_Computer_Chess_Championship "North American Computer Chess Championship"), was held, followed in 1974 by the first [World Computer Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Computer_Chess_Championship "World Computer Chess Championship"). In the late 1970s, dedicated home chess computers such as Fidelity Electronics' _[Chess Challenger](/w/index.php?title=Chess_Challenger&action=edit&redlink=1 "Chess Challenger (page does not exist)")_ became commercially available, as well as software to run on home computers. The overall standard of computer chess was low, however, until the 1990s.

The first [endgame tablebases](/wiki/Endgame_tablebases "Endgame tablebases"), which provided [perfect play](/wiki/Perfect_play "Perfect play") for relatively simple endgames such as king and rook versus king and bishop, appeared in the late 1970s. This set a precedent to the complete six- and seven-piece tablebases that became available in the 2000s and 2010s respectively.[[101]](#cite_note-106)

The first commercial [chess database](/wiki/Chess_database "Chess database"), a collection of chess games searchable by move and position, was introduced by the German company [ChessBase](/wiki/ChessBase "ChessBase") in 1987. Databases containing millions of chess games have since had a profound effect on opening theory and other areas of chess research.

Digital [chess clocks](/wiki/Chess_clocks "Chess clocks") were invented in 1973, though they did not become commonplace until the 1990s. Digital clocks allow for time controls involving [increments and delays](/wiki/Time_control#Increment_and_delay_methods "Time control").

### 1990–present: Rise of computers and online chess

#### Technology

The [Internet](/wiki/Internet "Internet") enabled [online chess](/wiki/Online_chess "Online chess") as a new medium of playing, with [chess servers](/wiki/Chess_servers "Chess servers") allowing users to play other people from different parts of the world in real time. The first such server, known as Internet Chess Server or ICS, was developed at the University of Utah in 1992. ICS formed the basis for the first commercial chess server, the [Internet Chess Club](/wiki/Internet_Chess_Club "Internet Chess Club"), which was launched in 1995, and for other early chess servers such as FICS (Free Internet Chess Server). Since then, many other platforms have appeared, and online chess began to rival over-the-board chess in popularity.[[102]](#cite_note-107)[[103]](#cite_note-108) During the 2020 [COVID-19 pandemic](/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic "COVID-19 pandemic"), the isolation ensuing from [quarantines](/wiki/Quarantine "Quarantine") imposed in many places around the world, combined with the success of the popular [Netflix](/wiki/Netflix "Netflix") show _[The Queen's Gambit](/wiki/The_Queen%27s_Gambit_(miniseries) "The Queen's Gambit (miniseries)")_ and other factors such as the popularity of online tournaments (notably [PogChamps](/wiki/PogChamps "PogChamps")) and chess [Twitch](/wiki/Twitch_(service) "Twitch (service)") streamers, resulted in a surge of popularity not only for online chess, but for the game of chess in general; this phenomenon has been referred to in the media as the 2020 online chess boom.[[104]](#cite_note-109)[[105]](#cite_note-110)

[Computer chess](/wiki/Computer_chess "Computer chess") has also seen major advances. By the 1990s, chess engines could consistently defeat most amateurs, and in 1997 [Deep Blue](/wiki/Deep_Blue_(chess_computer) "Deep Blue (chess computer)") defeated World Champion Garry Kasparov in [a six-game match](/wiki/Deep_Blue_versus_Garry_Kasparov "Deep Blue versus Garry Kasparov"), starting an era of computer dominance at the highest level of chess. In the 2010s, engines of superhuman strength became accessible for free on a number of [PC](/wiki/Personal_computer "Personal computer") and [mobile](/wiki/Mobile_phone "Mobile phone") platforms, and free engine analysis became a commonplace feature on internet chess servers. An adverse effect of the easy availability of engine analysis on hand-held devices and personal computers has been the rise of [computer cheating](/wiki/Chess_cheating "Chess cheating"), which has grown to be a major concern in both over-the-board and online chess.[[106]](#cite_note-111) In 2017, [AlphaZero](/wiki/AlphaZero "AlphaZero") – a [neural network](/wiki/Neural_network "Neural network") also capable of playing [shogi](/wiki/Shogi "Shogi") and [Go](/wiki/Go_(game) "Go (game)") – was introduced. Since then, many chess engines based on neural network evaluation have been written, the best of which have surpassed the traditional "[brute-force](/wiki/Brute-force_search "Brute-force search")" engines. AlphaZero also introduced many novel ideas and ways of playing the game, which affected the style of play at the top level.[[107]](#cite_note-112)

As [endgame tablebases](/wiki/Endgame_tablebases "Endgame tablebases") developed, they began to provide [perfect play](/wiki/Perfect_play "Perfect play") in endgame positions in which the [game-theoretical](/wiki/Game_theory "Game theory") outcome was previously unknown, such as positions with king, queen and pawn against king and queen. In 1991, Lewis Stiller published a tablebase for select six-piece endgames,[[108]](#cite_note-113)[[109]](#cite_note-114) and by 2005, following the publication of [Nalimov](/wiki/Eugene_Nalimov "Eugene Nalimov") tablebases, all six-piece endgame positions were solved. In 2012, Lomonosov tablebases were published which solved all seven-piece endgame positions.[[110]](#cite_note-115) Use of tablebases enhances the performance of chess engines by providing definitive results in some branches of analysis.

Technological progress made in the 1990s and the 21st century has influenced the way that chess is studied at all levels, as well as the state of chess as a [spectator sport](/wiki/Spectator_sport "Spectator sport").

Previously, preparation at the professional level required an extensive chess library and several subscriptions to publications such as _[Chess Informant](/wiki/Chess_Informant "Chess Informant")_ to keep up with opening developments and study opponents' games. Today, preparation at the professional level involves the use of databases containing millions of games, and engines to analyze different opening variations and prepare novelties.[[111]](#cite_note-116) A number of online learning resources are also available for players of all levels, such as online courses, tactics trainers, and video lessons.[[112]](#cite_note-117)

Since the late 1990s, it has been possible to follow major international chess events online, the players' moves being relayed in real time. Sensory boards have been developed to enable automatic transmission of moves. Chess players will frequently run engines while watching these games, allowing them to quickly identify mistakes by the players and spot tactical opportunities. While in the past the moves have been relayed live, today chess organizers will often impose a half-hour delay as an anti-cheating measure. In the mid-to-late 2010s – and especially following the 2020 online boom – it became commonplace for [supergrandmasters](/wiki/Super_Grandmaster "Super Grandmaster"), such as [Hikaru Nakamura](/wiki/Hikaru_Nakamura "Hikaru Nakamura") and [Magnus Carlsen](/wiki/Magnus_Carlsen "Magnus Carlsen"), to [livestream](/wiki/Livestream "Livestream") chess content on platforms such as [Twitch](/wiki/Twitch_(service) "Twitch (service)").[[113]](#cite_note-118)[[114]](#cite_note-119) Also following the boom, online chess started being viewed as an [e-sport](/wiki/E-sport "E-sport"), with e-sport teams signing chess players for the first time in 2020.[[115]](#cite_note-120)

#### Growth

Organized chess even for young children has become common. FIDE holds world championships for age levels down to 8 years old. The largest tournaments, in number of players, are those held for children.[[116]](#cite_note-121)

The number of [grandmasters](/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess) "Grandmaster (chess)") and other chess professionals has also grown in the modern era. Kenneth Regan and Guy Haworth conducted research involving comparison of move choices by players of different levels and from different periods with the analysis of strong chess engines; they concluded that the increase in the number of grandmasters and higher Elo ratings of the top players reflect an actual increase in the average standard of play, rather than "rating inflation" or "title inflation".[[117]](#cite_note-122)

#### Professional chess

In 1993, Garry Kasparov and [Nigel Short](/wiki/Nigel_Short "Nigel Short") broke ties with FIDE to organize their own match for the title and formed a competing [Professional Chess Association](/wiki/Professional_Chess_Association "Professional Chess Association") (PCA). From then until 2006, there were two simultaneous World Championships and respective World Champions: the PCA or "classical" champions extending the Steinitzian tradition in which the current champion plays a challenger in a series of games, and the other following FIDE's new format of many players competing in a large knockout tournament to determine the champion. Kasparov lost his PCA title in [2000](/wiki/Classical_World_Chess_Championship_2000 "Classical World Chess Championship 2000") to [Vladimir Kramnik](/wiki/Vladimir_Kramnik "Vladimir Kramnik") of Russia.[[118]](#cite_note-123) Due to the complicated state of world chess politics and difficulties obtaining commercial sponsorships, Kasparov was never able to challenge for the title again. Despite this, he continued to dominate in top level tournaments and remained the world's [highest rated player](/wiki/List_of_FIDE_chess_world_number_ones "List of FIDE chess world number ones") until his retirement from competitive chess in 2005.

The [World Chess Championship 2006](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_2006 "World Chess Championship 2006"), in which Kramnik beat the FIDE World Champion [Veselin Topalov](/wiki/Veselin_Topalov "Veselin Topalov"), reunified the titles and made Kramnik the undisputed World Chess Champion.[[119]](#cite_note-124) In September 2007, he lost the title to [Viswanathan Anand](/wiki/Viswanathan_Anand "Viswanathan Anand") of India, who won the [championship tournament](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_2007 "World Chess Championship 2007") in [Mexico City](/wiki/Mexico_City "Mexico City"). Anand defended his title in the [revenge match of 2008](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_2008 "World Chess Championship 2008"),[[120]](#cite_note-125) 2010 and 2012. [Magnus Carlsen](/wiki/Magnus_Carlsen "Magnus Carlsen") defeated Anand in the [2013 World Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_2013 "World Chess Championship 2013"), and defended his title in 2014, 2016, 2018, and 2021. After the 2021 match, he announced that he would not defend his title a fifth time, so the [2023 World Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Chess_Championship_2023 "World Chess Championship 2023") was played between the winner and runner-up of the [Candidates Tournament 2022](/wiki/Candidates_Tournament_2022 "Candidates Tournament 2022"): respectively, [Ian Nepomniachtchi](/wiki/Ian_Nepomniachtchi "Ian Nepomniachtchi") of Russia and [Ding Liren](/wiki/Ding_Liren "Ding Liren") of China. Ding beat Nepomniachtchi, making him the current World Chess Champion.[[11]](#cite_note-WCC2023-12)

## Connections

### Arts and humanities

Main article: [Chess in the arts](/wiki/Chess_in_the_arts "Chess in the arts")

In the [Middle Ages](/wiki/Middle_Ages "Middle Ages") and during the [Renaissance](/wiki/Renaissance "Renaissance"), chess was a part of [noble](/wiki/Nobility "Nobility") culture; it was used to teach war strategy and was dubbed the "[King's Game](/wiki/Chess_or_the_King%27s_Game "Chess or the King's Game")".[[121]](#cite_note-126) Gentlemen are "to be meanly seene in the play at Chestes", says the overview at the beginning of [Baldassare Castiglione](/wiki/Baldassare_Castiglione "Baldassare Castiglione")'s _[The Book of the Courtier](/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Courtier "The Book of the Courtier")_ (1528, English 1561 by Sir Thomas Hoby), but chess should not be a gentleman's main passion. Castiglione explains it further:

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Meister_der_Manessischen_Liederhandschrift_004.jpg/220px-Meister_der_Manessischen_Liederhandschrift_004.jpg)](/wiki/File:Meister_der_Manessischen_Liederhandschrift_004.jpg)

Noble chess players, Germany, c. 1320

> And what say you to the game at chestes? It is truely an honest kynde of enterteynmente and wittie, quoth Syr Friderick. But me think it hath a fault, whiche is, that a man may be to couning at it, for who ever will be excellent in the playe of chestes, I beleave he must beestowe much tyme about it, and applie it with so much study, that a man may assoone learne some noble scyence, or compase any other matter of importaunce, and yet in the ende in beestowing all that laboure, he knoweth no more but a game. Therfore in this I beleave there happeneth a very rare thing, namely, that the meane is more commendable, then the excellency.[[122]](#cite_note-127)

Some of the elaborate chess sets used by the aristocracy at least partially survive, such as the [Lewis chessmen](/wiki/Lewis_chessmen "Lewis chessmen").

Chess was often used as a basis of sermons on [morality](/wiki/Morality "Morality"). An example is _Liber de moribus hominum et officiis nobilium sive super ludo scacchorum_ ('Book of the customs of men and the duties of nobles or the Book of Chess'), written by an Italian [Dominican](/wiki/Order_of_Preachers "Order of Preachers") friar [Jacobus de Cessolis](/wiki/Jacobus_de_Cessolis "Jacobus de Cessolis") c. 1300. This book was one of the most popular of the Middle Ages.[[123]](#cite_note-128) The work was translated into many other languages (the first printed edition was published at Utrecht in 1473) and was the basis for [William Caxton](/wiki/William_Caxton "William Caxton")'s _The Game and Playe of the Chesse_ (1474), one of the first books printed in English.[[124]](#cite_note-129) Different chess pieces were used as metaphors for different classes of people, and human duties were derived from the rules of the game or from visual properties of the chess pieces:[[125]](#cite_note-130)

> The knyght ought to be made alle armed upon an hors in suche wyse that he haue an helme on his heed and a spere in his ryght hande/ and coueryd wyth his sheld/ a swerde and a mace on his lyft syde/ Cladd wyth an hawberk and plates to fore his breste/ legge harnoys on his legges/ Spores on his heelis on his handes his gauntelettes/ his hors well broken and taught and apte to bataylle and couerid with his armes/ whan the knyghtes ben maad they ben bayned or bathed/ that is the signe that they shold lede a newe lyf and newe maners/ also they wake alle the nyght in prayers and orysons vnto god that he wylle gyue hem grace that they may gete that thynge that they may not gete by nature/ The kynge or prynce gyrdeth a boute them a swerde in signe/ that they shold abyde and kepe hym of whom they take theyr dispenses and dignyte.[[126]](#cite_note-131)

Known in the circles of clerics, students, and merchants, chess entered into the popular culture of the Middle Ages. An example is the 209th song of [Carmina Burana](/wiki/Carmina_Burana "Carmina Burana") from the 13th century, which starts with the names of chess pieces, _Roch, pedites, regina..._[[127]](#cite_note-132) The game of chess, at times, has been discouraged by various religious authorities in Middle Ages: Jewish,[[128]](#cite_note-133) Catholic and [Orthodox](/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church "Eastern Orthodox Church").[[129]](#cite_note-134) Some Muslim authorities prohibited it even recently, for example [Ruhollah Khomeini](/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini "Ruhollah Khomeini") in 1979 and [Abdul-Aziz ash-Sheikh](/wiki/Abdul-Aziz_ibn_Abdullah_Al_ash-Sheikh "Abdul-Aziz ibn Abdullah Al ash-Sheikh") even later.[[130]](#cite_note-Forbidden-135)

During the [Age of Enlightenment](/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment "Age of Enlightenment"), chess was viewed as a means of self-improvement. [Benjamin Franklin](/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin "Benjamin Franklin"), in his article "[The Morals of Chess](/wiki/The_Morals_of_Chess "The Morals of Chess")" (1750), wrote:

> The Game of Chess is not merely an idle amusement; several very valuable qualities of the mind, useful in the course of human life, are to be acquired and strengthened by it, so as to become habits ready on all occasions; for life is a kind of Chess, in which we have often points to gain, and competitors or adversaries to contend with, and in which there is a vast variety of good and ill events, that are, in some degree, the effect of prudence, or the want of it. By playing at Chess then, we may learn:
> 
> **I. Foresight**, which looks a little into futurity, and considers the consequences that may attend an action ...
> 
> **II. Circumspection**, which surveys the whole Chess-board, or scene of action: – the relation of the several Pieces, and their situations ...
> 
> **III. Caution**, not to make our moves too hastily ...[[131]](#cite_note-136)

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Red_King_sleeping.jpg/240px-Red_King_sleeping.jpg)](/wiki/File:Red_King_sleeping.jpg)

_[Through the Looking-Glass](/wiki/Through_the_Looking-Glass "Through the Looking-Glass")_: the Red King is snoring. Illustration by Sir [John Tenniel](/wiki/John_Tenniel "John Tenniel").

Chess was occasionally criticized in the 19th century as a waste of time.[[132]](#cite_note-137)[[133]](#cite_note-138)

Chess is taught to children in schools around the world today. Many schools host chess clubs, and there are many scholastic tournaments specifically for children. Tournaments are held regularly in many countries, hosted by organizations such as the [United States Chess Federation](/wiki/United_States_Chess_Federation "United States Chess Federation") and the National Scholastic Chess Foundation.[[134]](#cite_note-139)

Chess is many times depicted in [the arts](/wiki/Chess_in_the_arts_and_literature "Chess in the arts and literature"); significant works where chess plays a key role range from Thomas Middleton's _[A Game at Chess](/wiki/A_Game_at_Chess "A Game at Chess")_ to _[Through the Looking-Glass](/wiki/Through_the_Looking-Glass "Through the Looking-Glass")_ by Lewis Carroll, to Vladimir Nabokov's _[The Defense](/wiki/The_Defense "The Defense")_, to _[The Royal Game](/wiki/The_Royal_Game "The Royal Game")_ by Stefan Zweig. Chess has also featured in film classics such as [Ingmar Bergman](/wiki/Ingmar_Bergman "Ingmar Bergman")'s _[The Seventh Seal](/wiki/The_Seventh_Seal "The Seventh Seal")_, [Satyajit Ray](/wiki/Satyajit_Ray "Satyajit Ray")'s _[The Chess Players](/wiki/Shatranj_Ke_Khilari "Shatranj Ke Khilari")_, and [Powell and Pressburger](/wiki/Powell_and_Pressburger "Powell and Pressburger")'s _[A Matter of Life and Death](/wiki/A_Matter_of_Life_and_Death_(film) "A Matter of Life and Death (film)")_.

Chess is also present in contemporary popular culture. For example, the characters in _[Star Trek](/wiki/Star_Trek "Star Trek")_ play a futuristic version of the game called "[Federation](/wiki/Federation_(Star_Trek) "Federation (Star Trek)") [Tri-Dimensional Chess](/wiki/Tri-Dimensional_Chess "Tri-Dimensional Chess")"[[135]](#cite_note-140) and "[Wizard's Chess](/wiki/Wizard%27s_Chess "Wizard's Chess")" is played in J.K. Rowling's _[Harry Potter](/wiki/Harry_Potter "Harry Potter")_.[[136]](#cite_note-Mayes-Elma2006-141)

### Mathematics

See also: [Mathematical chess problem](/wiki/Mathematical_chess_problem "Mathematical chess problem") and [Solving chess](/wiki/Solving_chess "Solving chess")

The game structure and nature of chess are related to several branches of mathematics. Many [combinatorical](/wiki/Combinatorics "Combinatorics") and [topological](/wiki/Topology "Topology") problems connected to chess, such as the [knight's tour](/wiki/Knight%27s_tour "Knight's tour") and the [eight queens puzzle](/wiki/Eight_queens_puzzle "Eight queens puzzle"), have been known for hundreds of years.

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Knight%27s_tour.svg/180px-Knight%27s_tour.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Knight%27s_tour.svg)

Mathematicians [Euler](/wiki/Euler "Euler"), [Legendre](/wiki/Adrien-Marie_Legendre "Adrien-Marie Legendre"), [de Moivre](/wiki/De_Moivre "De Moivre"), and [Vandermonde](/wiki/Vandermonde "Vandermonde") studied the [knight's tour](/wiki/Knight%27s_tour "Knight's tour").

The number of legal positions in chess is estimated to be 4.59 ± 0.38 × 1044 with a 95% confidence level,[[137]](#cite_note-142) with a [game-tree complexity](/wiki/Game-tree_complexity "Game-tree complexity") of approximately 10123. The game-tree complexity of chess was first calculated by [Claude Shannon](/wiki/Claude_Shannon "Claude Shannon") as 10120, a number known as the [Shannon number](/wiki/Shannon_number "Shannon number").[[138]](#cite_note-143) An average position typically has thirty to forty possible moves, but there may be as few as zero (in the case of checkmate or stalemate) or (in a constructed position) as many as 218.[[139]](#cite_note-144)

In 1913, [Ernst Zermelo](/wiki/Ernst_Zermelo "Ernst Zermelo") used chess as a basis for his theory of game strategies, which is considered one of the predecessors of [game theory](/wiki/Game_theory "Game theory").[[140]](#cite_note-145) [Zermelo's theorem](/wiki/Zermelo%27s_theorem_(game_theory) "Zermelo's theorem (game theory)") states that it is possible to [solve chess](/wiki/Solving_chess "Solving chess"), i.e. to determine with certainty the outcome of a perfectly played game (either White can force a win, or Black can force a win, or both sides can force at least a draw).[[141]](#cite_note-146) With 1043 legal positions in chess, however, it will take an impossibly long time to compute a perfect strategy with any feasible technology.[[142]](#cite_note-147)

### Psychology

There is an extensive scientific literature on chess psychology.[[note 6]](#cite_note-149)[[144]](#cite_note-150)[[145]](#cite_note-151)[[146]](#cite_note-152)[[147]](#cite_note-153) [Alfred Binet](/wiki/Alfred_Binet "Alfred Binet") and others showed that [knowledge](/wiki/Knowledge "Knowledge") and verbal, rather than visuospatial, ability lies at the core of expertise.[[148]](#cite_note-154)[[149]](#cite_note-155) In his doctoral thesis, [Adriaan de Groot](/wiki/Adriaan_de_Groot "Adriaan de Groot") showed that chess masters can rapidly perceive the key features of a position.[[150]](#cite_note-156) According to de Groot, this [perception](/wiki/Perception "Perception"), made possible by years of practice and study, is more important than the sheer ability to anticipate moves. De Groot showed that chess masters can memorize positions shown for a few seconds almost perfectly. The ability to memorize does not alone account for chess-playing skill, since masters and novices, when faced with random arrangements of chess pieces, had equivalent recall (about six positions in each case). Rather, it is the ability to recognize patterns, which are then memorized, which distinguished the skilled players from the novices. When the positions of the pieces were taken from an actual game, the masters had almost total positional recall.[[151]](#cite_note-157)

More recent research has focused on [chess as mental training](/wiki/Chess_as_mental_training "Chess as mental training"); the respective roles of knowledge and look-ahead search; [brain imaging](/wiki/Brain_imaging "Brain imaging") studies of chess masters and novices; [blindfold chess](/wiki/Blindfold_chess "Blindfold chess"); the role of [personality](/wiki/Personality_psychology "Personality psychology") and [intelligence](/wiki/Intelligence "Intelligence") in chess skill; gender differences; and computational models of chess expertise. The role of practice and talent in the development of chess and other domains of expertise has led to much empirical investigation. Ericsson and colleagues have argued that deliberate practice is sufficient for reaching high levels of expertise in chess.[[152]](#cite_note-158) Recent research, however, fails to replicate their results and indicates that factors other than practice are also important.[[153]](#cite_note-159)[[154]](#cite_note-Gobet-Fernand2011-160) For example, [Fernand Gobet](/wiki/Fernand_Gobet "Fernand Gobet") and colleagues have shown that stronger players started playing chess at a young age and that experts born in the Northern Hemisphere are more likely to have been born in late winter and early spring. Compared to the general population, chess players are more likely to be non-right-handed, though they found no correlation between handedness and skill.[[154]](#cite_note-Gobet-Fernand2011-160)

A relationship between chess skill and intelligence has long been discussed in scientific literature as well as in popular culture. Academic studies that investigate the relationship date back at least to 1927.[[155]](#cite_note-161) Although one meta-analysis and most children studies find a positive correlation between general cognitive ability and chess skill, adult studies show mixed results.[[156]](#cite_note-:0-162)[[157]](#cite_note-163)

## Composition

Richard Réti  
_[Ostrauer](/wiki/Ostrava "Ostrava") Morgenzeitung_, 1921

|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
||a|b|c|d|e|f|g|h||
|8|![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Chessboard480.svg/208px-Chessboard480.svg.png)<br><br>![h8 white king](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Chess_klt45.svg/26px-Chess_klt45.svg.png)<br><br>![a6 black king](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Chess_kdt45.svg/26px-Chess_kdt45.svg.png)<br><br>![c6 white pawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Chess_plt45.svg/26px-Chess_plt45.svg.png)<br><br>![h5 black pawn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Chess_pdt45.svg/26px-Chess_pdt45.svg.png)|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |8|
|7|7|
|6|6|
|5|5|
|4|4|
|3|3|
|2|2|
|1|1|
||a|b|c|d|e|f|g|h||

White to move and draw

This [Réti endgame study](/wiki/R%C3%A9ti_endgame_study "Réti endgame study") is solved by a [diagonal](/wiki/Diagonal "Diagonal") advance of the white king that brings it to both pawns simultaneously to stop the black pawn or to support the white pawn on its way to [queen](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess#queening "Glossary of chess").[[158]](#cite_note-164)

Main article: [Chess problem](/wiki/Chess_problem "Chess problem")

Chess composition is the art of creating chess problems (also called chess compositions). The creator is known as a [chess composer](/wiki/Chess_composer "Chess composer").[[159]](#cite_note-165) There are many types of chess problems; the two most important are:

- _[Directmates](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess_problems#directmate "Glossary of chess problems"):_ White to move first and checkmate Black within a specified number of moves, against any defense. These are often referred to as "mate in _n_" – for example "mate in three" (a _three-mover_); two- and three-move problems are the most common. These usually involve positions that would be highly unlikely to occur in an actual game, and are intended to illustrate a particular _[theme](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess_problems#theme "Glossary of chess problems")_, usually requiring a surprising or counterintuitive _[key](/wiki/Glossary_of_chess_problems#key "Glossary of chess problems")_ move. Themes associated with chess problems occasionally appear in actual games, when they are referred to as "problem-like" moves.[[160]](#cite_note-166)
- _[Studies](/wiki/Endgame_study "Endgame study"):_ orthodox problems where the stipulation is that White to play must win or draw. The majority of studies are [endgame](/wiki/Chess_endgame "Chess endgame") positions.[[161]](#cite_note-167)

[Fairy chess](/wiki/Fairy_chess "Fairy chess") is a branch of chess problem composition involving altered rules, such as the use of unconventional pieces or boards, or unusual stipulations such as [reflexmates](/wiki/Reflexmate "Reflexmate").

Tournaments for composition and solving of chess problems are organized by the [World Federation for Chess Composition](/wiki/World_Federation_for_Chess_Composition "World Federation for Chess Composition"), which works cooperatively with but independent of FIDE. The WFCC awards titles for composing and solving chess problems.[[162]](#cite_note-168)

## Online chess

Main article: [Online chess](/wiki/Online_chess "Online chess")

Online chess is chess that is played over the internet, allowing players to play against each other in real time. This is done through the use of [Internet chess servers](/wiki/Internet_chess_server "Internet chess server"), which pair up individual players based on their rating using an [Elo](/wiki/Elo_rating_system "Elo rating system") or similar rating system. Online chess saw a spike in growth during the [quarantines](/wiki/Quarantine "Quarantine") of the [COVID-19 pandemic](/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic "COVID-19 pandemic").[[163]](#cite_note-Ruiter-169)[[164]](#cite_note-businessinsider-170) This can be attributed to both isolation and the popularity of [Netflix](/wiki/Netflix "Netflix") miniseries _[The Queen's Gambit](/wiki/The_Queen%27s_Gambit_(miniseries) "The Queen's Gambit (miniseries)")_, which was released in October 2020.[[163]](#cite_note-Ruiter-169)[[164]](#cite_note-businessinsider-170) Chess app downloads on the [App Store](/wiki/App_Store_(iOS/iPadOS) "App Store (iOS/iPadOS)") and [Google Play Store](/wiki/Google_Play_Store "Google Play Store") rose by 63% after the show debuted.[[165]](#cite_note-morningbrew-171) [Chess.com](/wiki/Chess.com "Chess.com") saw more than twice as many account registrations in November as it had in previous months, and the number of games played monthly on Lichess doubled as well. There was also a demographic shift in players, with female registration on Chess.com shifting from 22% to 27% of new players.[[166]](#cite_note-172) [GM](/wiki/Grandmaster_(chess) "Grandmaster (chess)") [Maurice Ashley](/wiki/Maurice_Ashley "Maurice Ashley") said "A boom is taking place in chess like we have never seen maybe since the Bobby Fischer days", attributing the growth to an increased desire to do something constructive during the pandemic.[[167]](#cite_note-173) [USCF](/wiki/United_States_Chess_Federation "United States Chess Federation") Women's Program Director [Jennifer Shahade](/wiki/Jennifer_Shahade "Jennifer Shahade") stated that chess works well on the internet, since pieces do not need to be reset and [matchmaking](/wiki/Matchmaking_(video_games) "Matchmaking (video games)") is virtually instant.[[168]](#cite_note-174)

## Computer chess

Main article: [Computer chess](/wiki/Computer_chess "Computer chess")

See also: [Human–computer chess matches](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_chess_matches "Human–computer chess matches"), [Deep Blue versus Garry Kasparov](/wiki/Deep_Blue_versus_Garry_Kasparov "Deep Blue versus Garry Kasparov"), and [Chess engine](/wiki/Chess_engine "Chess engine")

The idea of creating a chess-playing machine dates to the 18th century; around 1769, the chess-playing [automaton](/wiki/Automaton "Automaton") called [The Turk](/wiki/Mechanical_Turk "Mechanical Turk") became famous before being exposed as a [hoax](/wiki/Hoax "Hoax").[[169]](#cite_note-175) Serious trials based on automata, such as [El Ajedrecista](/wiki/El_Ajedrecista "El Ajedrecista"), were too complex and limited to be useful. Since the advent of the [digital computer](/wiki/Digital_computer "Digital computer") in the 1950s, chess enthusiasts, [computer engineers](/wiki/Computer_engineer "Computer engineer"), and computer scientists have built, with increasing degrees of seriousness and success, chess-playing machines and computer programs.[[170]](#cite_note-176) The groundbreaking paper on computer chess, "Programming a Computer for Playing Chess", was published in 1950 by [Claude Shannon](/wiki/Claude_Shannon "Claude Shannon").[[note 7]](#cite_note-178) He wrote:

> The chess machine is an ideal one to start with, since: (1) the problem is sharply defined both in allowed operations (the moves) and in the ultimate goal (checkmate); (2) it is neither so simple as to be trivial nor too difficult for satisfactory solution; (3) chess is generally considered to require "thinking" for skillful play; a solution of this problem will force us either to admit the possibility of a mechanized thinking or to further restrict our concept of "thinking"; (4) the discrete structure of chess fits well into the digital nature of modern computers.[[172]](#cite_note-179)

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/RS_Chess_Computer.JPG/180px-RS_Chess_Computer.JPG)](/wiki/File:RS_Chess_Computer.JPG)

1990s chess-playing computer

The [Association for Computing Machinery](/wiki/Association_for_Computing_Machinery "Association for Computing Machinery") (ACM) held the first major chess tournament for computers, the [North American Computer Chess Championship](/wiki/North_American_Computer_Chess_Championship "North American Computer Chess Championship"), in September 1970. [CHESS 3.0](/wiki/Chess_(Northwestern_University) "Chess (Northwestern University)"), a chess program from [Northwestern University](/wiki/Northwestern_University "Northwestern University"), won the championship. The first [World Computer Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Computer_Chess_Championship "World Computer Chess Championship"), held in 1974, was won by the Soviet program [Kaissa](/wiki/Kaissa "Kaissa"). At first considered only a curiosity, the best [chess playing programs](/wiki/Chess_engine "Chess engine") have become extremely strong. In 1997, a computer won a chess match using classical time controls against a reigning World Champion for the first time: [IBM's](/wiki/IBM "IBM") [Deep Blue](/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue "IBM Deep Blue") beat [Garry Kasparov](/wiki/Garry_Kasparov "Garry Kasparov") 3½–2½ (it scored two wins, one loss, and three [draws](/wiki/Draw_(chess) "Draw (chess)")).[[173]](#cite_note-180)[[174]](#cite_note-181) There was some controversy over [the match](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_chess_matches#1997 "Human–computer chess matches"),[[175]](#cite_note-182) and [human–computer matches](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_chess_matches "Human–computer chess matches") were relatively close over the next few years, until convincing computer victories in [2005](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_chess_matches#Hydra–Adams_(2005) "Human–computer chess matches") and in [2006](/wiki/Human%E2%80%93computer_chess_matches#Kramnik–Deep_Fritz_(2006) "Human–computer chess matches").

In 2009, a [mobile phone](/wiki/Mobile_phone "Mobile phone") won a [category](/wiki/Category_(chess_tournament) "Category (chess tournament)") 6 tournament with a performance rating of 2898: chess engine [Hiarcs](/wiki/Hiarcs "Hiarcs") 13 running on the mobile phone [HTC Touch HD](/wiki/HTC_Touch_HD "HTC Touch HD") won the Copa Mercosur tournament with nine wins and one draw.[[176]](#cite_note-183) The best chess programs are now able to consistently beat the strongest human players, to the extent that human–computer matches no longer attract interest from chess players or the media.[[177]](#cite_note-184) While the [World Computer Chess Championship](/wiki/World_Computer_Chess_Championship "World Computer Chess Championship") still exists, the [Top Chess Engine Championship](/wiki/Top_Chess_Engine_Championship "Top Chess Engine Championship") (TCEC) is widely regarded as the unofficial world championship for chess [engines](/wiki/Chess_engine "Chess engine").[[178]](#cite_note-185)[[179]](#cite_note-186)[[180]](#cite_note-187) The current champion is [Stockfish](/wiki/Stockfish_(chess) "Stockfish (chess)").

With huge databases of past games and high analytical ability, computers can help players to learn chess and prepare for matches. [Internet Chess Servers](/wiki/Internet_Chess_Server "Internet Chess Server") allow people to find and play opponents worldwide. The presence of computers and modern communication tools have raised concerns regarding [cheating during games](/wiki/Cheating_in_chess "Cheating in chess").[[181]](#cite_note-188)

## Variants

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chess//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Sittuyin_starting_position_No._8.PNG/220px-Sittuyin_starting_position_No._8.PNG)](/wiki/File:Sittuyin_starting_position_No._8.PNG)

[Sittuyin](/wiki/Sittuyin "Sittuyin"), after setup phase. Players elect their own starting setups behind the pawns.

Main articles: [Chess variant](/wiki/Chess_variant "Chess variant") and [List of chess variants](/wiki/List_of_chess_variants "List of chess variants")

There are more than two thousand published chess variants, games with similar but different rules.[[182]](#cite_note-189) Most of them are of relatively recent origin.[[183]](#cite_note-190) They include:

- direct predecessors of chess, such as [chaturanga](/wiki/Chaturanga "Chaturanga") and [shatranj](/wiki/Shatranj "Shatranj");
- traditional national or regional games that share common ancestors with Western chess such as [xiangqi](/wiki/Xiangqi "Xiangqi") (Chinese chess), [shogi](/wiki/Shogi "Shogi") (Japanese chess), [janggi](/wiki/Janggi "Janggi") (Korean chess), [makruk](/wiki/Makruk "Makruk") (Thai chess), [sittuyin](/wiki/Sittuyin "Sittuyin") (Burmese chess), and [shatar](/wiki/Shatar "Shatar") (Mongolian chess);
- modern variations employing different rules (e.g. [Losing chess](/wiki/Losing_chess "Losing chess") or [Chess960](/wiki/Chess960 "Chess960")[[note 8]](#cite_note-192)), different forces (e.g. [Dunsany's Chess](/wiki/Dunsany%27s_Chess "Dunsany's Chess")), [non-standard pieces](/wiki/Fairy_piece "Fairy piece") (e.g. [Grand Chess](/wiki/Grand_Chess "Grand Chess")), and different board geometries (e.g. [hexagonal chess](/wiki/Hexagonal_chess "Hexagonal chess") or [Infinite chess](/wiki/Infinite_chess "Infinite chess"));

In the context of chess variants, regular (i.e. FIDE) chess is commonly referred to as _Western chess_, _international chess_, _orthodox chess_, _orthochess_, and _classic chess_.[[185]](#cite_note-193)[[186]](#cite_note-194)

3. **[^](#cite_ref-57 "Jump up")** At that time the Spanish word would have been written _axedrez_. The Spanish "x" was pronounced as English "sh", as the Portuguese "x" still is today. The spelling of _ajedrez_ changed after [Spanish lost the "sh" sound](/wiki/Spanish_orthography#History "Spanish orthography").
4. **[^](#cite_ref-70 "Jump up")** The allegorical poem [Scachs d'amor](/wiki/Scachs_d%27amor "Scachs d'amor"), the first to describe a modern game, is probably from 1475.[[65]](#cite_note-68)[[66]](#cite_note-69)
5. **[^](#cite_ref-94 "Jump up")** This is stated in _The Encyclopaedia of Chess_ (1970, p. 223) by [Anne Sunnucks](/wiki/Anne_Sunnucks "Anne Sunnucks"), but is disputed by [Edward Winter (chess historian)](/wiki/Edward_Winter_(chess_historian) "Edward Winter (chess historian)") in his [Chess Notes 5144 and 5152](http://www.chesshistory.com/winter/winter38.html).
6. **[^](#cite_ref-149 "Jump up")** Chess is even called the "[drosophila](/wiki/Drosophila "Drosophila")" of [cognitive psychology](/wiki/Cognitive_psychology "Cognitive psychology") and artificial intelligence ([AI](/wiki/AI "AI")) studies, because it represents the domain in which expert performance has been most intensively studied and measured.[[143]](#cite_note-148)
7. **[^](#cite_ref-178 "Jump up")** [Alan Turing](/wiki/Alan_Turing "Alan Turing") made an attempt in 1953.[[171]](#cite_note-177)
8. **[^](#cite_ref-192 "Jump up")** In 2008 FIDE added Chess960 rules to an appendix of the Handbook.[[184]](#cite_note-FideLawsOfChess2009-191) This section is now classified under "Guidelines",[[1]](#cite_note-FideLawsOfChess-1) indicating that the rules presented do not have the weight of FIDE law.

186. **[^](#cite_ref-194 "Jump up")** Schmittberger, R. Wayne (1992). [_New Rules for Classic Games_](https://archive.org/details/newrulesforclass00rway/page/186). John Wiley & Sons Inc. p. [186](https://archive.org/details/newrulesforclass00rway/page/186). [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-471-53621-5](/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-471-53621-5 "Special:BookSources/978-0-471-53621-5"). The form of chess most people know – which is sometimes referred to as Western chess, orthodox chess, or orthochess – is itself just one of many that have been played throughout history.
